<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">UEFA Euro 1996</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

<p>The <b>1996 UEFA European Football Championship</b>, commonly referred to as <b>Euro 96</b>, was the 10th <a href="/wiki/UEFA_European_Championship" title="UEFA European Championship">UEFA European Championship</a>, a quadrennial <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> tournament contested by European nations and organised by <a href="/wiki/UEFA" title="UEFA">UEFA</a>. It took place in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> from 8 to 30 June 1996.</p>
<p>It was the first European Championship to feature 16 finalists, following UEFA's decision to expand the tournament from eight teams. Games were staged in eight cities and, although not all games were sold out, the tournament holds the European Championship's second-highest aggregate attendance (1,276,000) and average per game (41,158) for the 16-team format,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> surpassed only in <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro_2012" title="UEFA Euro 2012">2012</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2012crowds_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-2012crowds-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Germany_national_football_team" title="Germany national football team">Germany</a> won the tournament, beating the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic_national_football_team" title="Czech Republic national football team">Czech Republic</a> 2–1 in the final with a <a href="/wiki/Golden_goal#Association_football" title="Golden goal">golden goal</a> during extra time; this was the first major competition to be decided using this method. This was also Germany's first major title won as a <a href="/wiki/German_reunification" title="German reunification">unified nation</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bidding_process">Bidding process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Bidding process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>At the time of the bidding process, it had not yet been confirmed that sixteen teams would be participating. Instead, the bids were largely prepared as if hosting an eight-team tournament, meaning only four venues were due to be required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> All candidates had to submit their plans by 10 December 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The hosting of the event was contested by five bids: Austria, England, Greece, the Netherlands and Portugal. The English bid was selected by the UEFA Executive Committee at a meeting in <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a> on 5 May 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bid_decision_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bid_decision-5">[5]</a></sup> In the year preceding the decision, the <a href="/wiki/The_Football_Association" title="The Football Association">English FA</a> had dropped out plans to also <a href="/wiki/1998_FIFA_World_Cup#Host_selection" title="1998 FIFA World Cup">bid for the 1998 World Cup</a> in order to gain the support of other UEFA members who were planning to bid for that event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bid_decision_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bid_decision-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Summary">Summary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Summary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_round">First round</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: First round">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The hosts, England, drew 1–1 with <a href="/wiki/Switzerland_national_football_team" title="Switzerland national football team">Switzerland</a> in the opening match of Group A when <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shearer" title="Alan Shearer">Alan Shearer</a>'s 23rd-minute goal was equalled by a late <a href="/wiki/Kubilay_T%C3%BCrkyilmaz" title="Kubilay Türkyilmaz">Kubilay Türkyilmaz</a> penalty kick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> England defeated rivals Scotland 2–0 in their next game, and then produced one of their finest performances ever with a 4–1 win over the Netherlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Kluivert" title="Patrick Kluivert">Patrick Kluivert</a>'s late goal for the Netherlands secured his team second place in the group and ensured that Scotland would exit another major competition on goal difference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scotland-holland_euro_96.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="552" data-file-width="800" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Scotland-holland_euro_96.jpg/220px-Scotland-holland_euro_96.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Scotland-holland_euro_96.jpg/330px-Scotland-holland_euro_96.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Scotland-holland_euro_96.jpg/440px-Scotland-holland_euro_96.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scotland-holland_euro_96.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro_1996#Group_A" title="UEFA Euro 1996">Group A</a> game <a href="/wiki/Scotland_national_football_team" title="Scotland national football team">Scotland</a> v <a href="/wiki/Netherlands_national_football_team" title="Netherlands national football team">Netherlands</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Villa_Park_(stadium)" title="Villa Park (stadium)">Villa Park</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Group B had Western European France and Spain, along with Balkan World Cup participants Romania and Bulgaria. France and Spain dominated the group,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> with France avenging Bulgaria for the 1994 qualification debacle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> and World Cup quarter-finalists Romania going home,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> with no points and only one goal scored.</p>
<p>Groups C and D saw the Czech Republic and Croatia, whose national teams had only recently come into existence, qualify for the knockout stages. The Czechs lost to Germany, the eventual group winners, in their opener, but then defeated Italy and drew with Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Italy's defeat meant they had to beat Germany in their final game to progress, but the <a href="/wiki/1994_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1994 FIFA World Cup">World Cup finalists</a> could only manage a 0–0 draw and were eliminated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> In Group D, Croatia qualified for the quarter-finals, with wins over Turkey (1–0) and Denmark (3–0).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The loss to the Croats ultimately sent the Danes, the surprise champions of <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro_1992" title="UEFA Euro 1992">1992</a>, home. Turkey became the first team since the introduction of a group stage to be eliminated without gaining a point or scoring a goal.</p>
<p>The other three quarter-finalists were Portugal (whose "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Generation" title="Golden Generation">Golden Generation</a>" was competing at its first major tournament), Spain, and a France team featuring a young <a href="/wiki/Zinedine_Zidane" title="Zinedine Zidane">Zinedine Zidane</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quarter-finals_and_semi-finals">Quarter-finals and semi-finals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Quarter-finals and semi-finals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The knockout stages were characterised by negative, defensive play; as a result, only nine goals were scored in the seven games and four of the matches were decided on penalties. The first quarter-final between the hosts and Spain ended goalless, after Spain had two goals disallowed and two claims for a penalty denied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The English progressed 4–2 on spot kicks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> France and Netherlands also played out a 0–0 draw, with France winning the penalty shootout 5–4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Klinsmann" title="Jürgen Klinsmann">Jürgen Klinsmann</a> opened the scoring for Germany in their match against Croatia. A goal from <a href="/wiki/Davor_%C5%A0uker" title="Davor Šuker">Davor Šuker</a> evened the score after 51 minutes, before <a href="/wiki/Matthias_Sammer" title="Matthias Sammer">Matthias Sammer</a> of Germany scored eight minutes later, and the game ended 2–1 to Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Czech Republic progressed after beating Portugal 1–0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wembley_Twin_Towers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="547" data-file-width="800" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Wembley_Twin_Towers.jpg/220px-Wembley_Twin_Towers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Wembley_Twin_Towers.jpg/330px-Wembley_Twin_Towers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Wembley_Twin_Towers.jpg/440px-Wembley_Twin_Towers.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wembley_Twin_Towers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View of <a href="/wiki/Wembley_Stadium_(1923)" title="Wembley Stadium (1923)">Wembley Stadium</a> from <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Way" title="Olympic Way">Wembley Way</a> before the <a href="/wiki/Germany_national_football_team" title="Germany national football team">Germany</a> v <a href="/wiki/England_national_football_team" title="England national football team">England</a> semi-final</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first semi-final, featuring France and Czech Republic, resulted in another 0–0 draw and penalties. <a href="/wiki/Reynald_Pedros" title="Reynald Pedros">Reynald Pedros</a> was the one player to miss in the shootout, as Czech Republic won the penalty shoot-out 6–5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The other semi-final was a repeat of the 1990 World Cup semi-final between Germany and England. Alan Shearer headed in, after three minutes to give his side the lead, but <a href="/wiki/Stefan_Kuntz" title="Stefan Kuntz">Stefan Kuntz</a> evened the score less than 15 minutes later, and the score remained 1–1 after 90 minutes. In extra time, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Gascoigne" title="Paul Gascoigne">Paul Gascoigne</a> came very close to scoring a golden goal, but fractionally missed a cross from Shearer in front of the empty goal, <a href="/wiki/Darren_Anderton" title="Darren Anderton">Darren Anderton</a> hit the post, and Kuntz had a goal disallowed for pushing. Neither team was able to find a second goal. In penalties, both sides scored their first five kicks, but in the sixth round, <a href="/wiki/Gareth_Southgate" title="Gareth Southgate">Gareth Southgate</a> had his penalty saved, allowing <a href="/wiki/Andreas_M%C3%B6ller" title="Andreas Möller">Andreas Möller</a> to score the winning goal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final">Final</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Final">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The final saw the Czech Republic hoping to repeat <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro_1976" title="UEFA Euro 1976">Euro 1976</a> when Czechoslovakia defeated West Germany; the Germans were aiming to win their third European Championship. <a href="/wiki/Patrik_Berger" title="Patrik Berger">Patrik Berger</a> scored from a penalty in 59th minute to put the Czechs ahead. German substitute <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Bierhoff" title="Oliver Bierhoff">Oliver Bierhoff</a> then scored to make it 1–1. Five minutes into extra time, Bierhoff's shot was mishandled by Czech goalkeeper Kouba<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and the ball ended up in the back of the net for the first <a href="/wiki/Golden_goal" title="Golden goal">golden goal</a> in the history of the competition. Germany were European champions again, the first time as a unified country.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Qualification">Qualification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Qualification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro_1996_qualifying" title="UEFA Euro 1996 qualifying">UEFA Euro 1996 qualifying</a></div>
<p>On 30 November 1992, UEFA formally decided to expand the tournament to sixteen teams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> UEFA cited the increased number of international teams following the recent break up of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Yugoslavia" title="Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a> – rising from 33 UEFA members in 1988 to 48 by 1994 – as a driving factor behind the expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Forty-seven teams ultimately entered to compete for the fifteen remaining places in the finals, alongside hosts England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The draw for the qualifying competition took place in <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a> on 22 January 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qualifying_draw_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Qualifying_draw-29">[29]</a></sup> The teams were divided into eight groups, each containing either six or five teams. The qualifying process began in April 1994 and concluded in December 1995. At the conclusion of the qualifying group stage in November 1994, the eight group winners qualified automatically, along with the six highest ranked second placed teams. The remaining two second placed teams – <a href="/wiki/Netherlands_national_football_team" title="Netherlands national football team">The Netherlands</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland_national_football_team" title="Republic of Ireland national football team">Republic of Ireland</a> – contested a one-off play-off match in England to decide the final qualifier.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Qualified_teams">Qualified teams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Qualified teams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro_1996_squads" title="UEFA Euro 1996 squads">UEFA Euro 1996 squads</a></div>

</table>
<p>With the extended format, three teams were able to qualify for their first European Championship: <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria_national_football_team" title="Bulgaria national football team">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland_national_football_team" title="Switzerland national football team">Switzerland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkey_national_football_team" title="Turkey national football team">Turkey</a>. <a href="/wiki/Croatia_national_football_team" title="Croatia national football team">Croatia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russia_national_football_team" title="Russia national football team">Russia</a> competed for the first time in their own right since the dissolution of Yugoslavia and the Soviet Union (though the Russian team is considered by FIFA to be the direct descendant of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union_national_football_team" title="Soviet Union national football team">Soviet Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/CIS_national_football_team" title="CIS national football team">CIS</a> teams that had appeared in six past tournaments). Seven of the eight participants at the previous tournament in 1992 were again present, with only <a href="/wiki/Sweden_national_football_team" title="Sweden national football team">Sweden</a> – despite also having finished third in the World Cup two years earlier – missing out.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_draw">Final draw</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Final draw">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The draw for the final tournament took place on 17 December 1995 at the <a href="/wiki/International_Convention_Centre,_Birmingham" title="International Convention Centre, Birmingham">International Convention Centre</a> in <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Final_draw_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Final_draw-35">[30]</a></sup> Only four teams were seeded: <a href="/wiki/England_national_football_team" title="England national football team">England</a> (as hosts), <a href="/wiki/Denmark_national_football_team" title="Denmark national football team">Denmark</a> (as holders), <a href="/wiki/Spain_national_football_team" title="Spain national football team">Spain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germany_national_football_team" title="Germany national football team">Germany</a>. The remaining twelve teams were all unseeded and could be drawn in any group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Final_draw_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Final_draw-35">[30]</a></sup></p>

<p>In the draw procedure, the unseeded teams were drawn one-by-one into each of the four groups before finally the four seeds were then drawn out. While it was decreed in advance that England's group would be Group A, the remaining three groups were then drawn out a letter to decide the name of their group, and therefore determine what venues they would play at.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Final_draw_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Final_draw-35">[30]</a></sup> The balls were drawn by UEFA figures <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Aigner" title="Gerhard Aigner">Gerhard Aigner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lennart_Johansson" title="Lennart Johansson">Lennart Johansson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Final_draw_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-Final_draw-35">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Venues">Venues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Venues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Since the implementation of the <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Report" title="Taylor Report">Taylor Report</a> in 1990, following the 1989 Hillsborough stadium disaster, England now had enough all-seater stadia of sufficient capacity to hold an expanded tournament due to the necessary stadium refurbishment by its leading clubs. The stadium capacities listed in the table are for the time of the tournament.</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center;">
<tr>
<td colspan="2" rowspan="6">
<center>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center;" width="340">
<tr>
<td align="center">
<div style="position: relative;border:0px solid black;width:260px;">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Match_ball">Match ball</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Match ball">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Adidas_Questra" title="Adidas Questra">Adidas Questra</a></div>
<p>A custom version of the <a href="/wiki/Adidas_Questra" title="Adidas Questra">Adidas Questra</a>, the <i>Questra Europa</i>, was the official match ball of the championships. The design of the ball included a reworking of the England badge, and was the first coloured ball in a major football tournament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[31]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Results">Results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Euro_1996.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1200" height="367" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Euro_1996.png/440px-Euro_1996.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Euro_1996.png/660px-Euro_1996.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Euro_1996.png/880px-Euro_1996.png 2x" width="440"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Euro_1996.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Finishing positions of the participating teams</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Group_stage">Group stage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Group stage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The teams finishing in the top two positions in each of the four groups progress to the quarter-finals, while the bottom two teams in each group were eliminated from the tournament. For the first time at a European Championship three points were awarded for a win, with one for a draw and a none for a defeat.</p>
<table class="wikitable collapsible collapsed">
<tr>
<th>Tie breaking criteria</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>
<ol>
<li>Higher number of points obtained in the matches played between the teams in question;</li>
<li>Superior goal difference resulting from the matches played between the teams in question (if more than two teams finish equal on points);</li>
<li>Higher number of goals scored in the matches played between the teams in question (if more than two teams finish equal on points);</li>
<li>If, after having applied criteria 1 to 3 to more than two teams, two teams still have an equal ranking, criteria 1 to 3 are reapplied exclusively to the matches between the two teams in question to determine the final rankings of the two teams. If this procedure does not lead to a decision, criteria 5 to 9 apply in the order given;</li>
<li>Superior goal difference in all group matches;</li>
<li>Higher number of goals scored in all group matches;</li>
<li>Position using UEFA's national team coefficient ranking system calculated using average points per game from: the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro_1992_qualifying" title="UEFA Euro 1992 qualifying">Euro 1992 qualifying stage</a> and <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro_1992" title="UEFA Euro 1992">final tournament</a>, the <a href="/wiki/1994_FIFA_World_Cup_qualification_(UEFA)" title="1994 FIFA World Cup qualification (UEFA)">1994 World Cup qualifying stage</a> and <a href="/wiki/1994_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1994 FIFA World Cup">final tournament</a> and the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro_1996_qualifying" title="UEFA Euro 1996 qualifying">Euro 1996 qualifying stage</a>.</li>
<li>Fair play conduct of the teams (final tournament);</li>
<li>Drawing of lots.</li>
</ol>
</td>
</tr>
</table>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Knockout_stage">Knockout stage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Knockout stage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro_1996_knockout_stage" title="UEFA Euro 1996 knockout stage">UEFA Euro 1996 knockout stage</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Format">Format</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Format">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The knockout stage was a <a href="/wiki/Single-elimination_tournament" title="Single-elimination tournament">single-elimination tournament</a> with each round eliminating the losers. Any game that was undecided by the end of the regular 90 minutes, was followed by up to thirty minutes of <a href="/wiki/Overtime_(sports)#Association_football" title="Overtime (sports)">extra time</a>. For the first time in a major football competition, the <a href="/wiki/Golden_goal#Association_football" title="Golden goal">golden goal system</a> was applied, whereby the first team to score during the extra time would become the winner. If no goal was scored there would be a <a href="/wiki/Penalty_shoot-out_(association_football)" title="Penalty shoot-out (association football)">penalty shoot-out</a> to determine the winner. For the first time the final was won by a golden goal.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistics">Statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Goalscorers">Goalscorers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Goalscorers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Alan Shearer received the Golden Boot award for scoring five goals. In total, 64 goals were scored by 48 different players, with only one of them credited as own goal.</p>
<dl>
<dt>5 goals</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/England" title="England"><img alt="England" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="800" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/23px-Flag_of_England.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/35px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/46px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shearer" title="Alan Shearer">Alan Shearer</a></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt>3 goals</dt>
</dl>
<div>
<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:50%;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 33.33%;text-align:left;vertical-align:top;">

<dl>
<dt>Golden Boot</dt>
</dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alan_Shearer" title="Alan Shearer">Alan Shearer</a> was awarded the Golden Boot award, after scoring five goals in the group stage and in the semi-finals against <a href="/wiki/Germany_national_football_team" title="Germany national football team">Germany</a>.</p>
<ul>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/England" title="England"><img alt="England" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="800" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/23px-Flag_of_England.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/35px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/46px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shearer" title="Alan Shearer">Alan Shearer</a> (5 goals)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-team_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-team-38">[33]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fastest_goal">Fastest goal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Fastest goal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>2 minutes and 14 seconds</b>: <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shearer" title="Alan Shearer">Alan Shearer</a> (<a href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro_1996_knockout_stage#England_vs_Germany" title="UEFA Euro 1996 knockout stage">England vs Germany</a>)</p>

<p>2.06 goals per game</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marketing">Marketing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Marketing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slogan_and_theme_songs">Slogan and theme songs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Slogan and theme songs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three-lions-original-1996.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="528" data-file-width="600" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/78/Three-lions-original-1996.jpg/200px-Three-lions-original-1996.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/78/Three-lions-original-1996.jpg/300px-Three-lions-original-1996.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/78/Three-lions-original-1996.jpg/400px-Three-lions-original-1996.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Three-lions-original-1996.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Three Lions single</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The competition slogan was <b>Football Comes Home</b> reflecting that the sport's rules were first standardised in the United Kingdom. UEFA President <a href="/wiki/Lennart_Johansson" title="Lennart Johansson">Lennart Johansson</a> had said that the organisation had felt it time to bring the event "back to the motherland of football".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qualifying_draw_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Qualifying_draw-29">[29]</a></sup> The slogan was incorporated into the competition's most popular song: "<a href="/wiki/Three_Lions" title="Three Lions">Three Lions</a>" recorded by comedians <a href="/wiki/David_Baddiel" title="David Baddiel">David Baddiel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Skinner" title="Frank Skinner">Frank Skinner</a> with <a href="/wiki/Britpop" title="Britpop">Britpop</a> band the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lightning_Seeds" title="Lightning Seeds">Lightning Seeds</a>. Baddiel and Skinner were then strongly connected with football owing to their BBC show <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_Football_League" title="Fantasy Football League">Fantasy Football League</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-song_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-song-39">[34]</a></sup> Released as a single, the song topped the <a href="/wiki/UK_Singles_Chart" title="UK Singles Chart">UK Singles Chart</a> for a total of two weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[35]</a></sup> It was promoted by a video featuring the England squad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-song_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-song-39">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>The song was prominently sung by England fans during all their games, and was also chanted by the German team upon parading the trophy in Berlin after the tournament. It was even referenced by future Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> in an address at the 1996 Labour Party Conference with the line: <i>"Seventeen years of hurt, never stopped us dreaming, Labour's coming home".</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite Three Lions being the song most strongly connected with the tournament, its official song was instead "We're in This Together" by <a href="/wiki/Simply_Red" title="Simply Red">Simply Red</a>. The song was performed at the tournament's opening ceremony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Merchandise_and_mascots">Merchandise and mascots</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Merchandise and mascots">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mint" title="Royal Mint">British Royal Mint</a> issued a commemorative <a href="/wiki/Two_pounds_(British_coin)" title="Two pounds (British coin)">£2 coin</a> in 1996, which featured a representation of a football, "1996" in the centre, and 16 small rings representing the 16 competing teams. Further special coins were only issued in the Isle of Man and Gibraltar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>The official mascot, 'Goaliath', was designed in a similar fashion to the original World Cup mascot from the <a href="/wiki/1966_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1966 FIFA World Cup">1966 World Cup</a>. Goaliath comprised a lion, the image on the English team crest, dressed in an England football strip and football boots whilst holding a football under his right arm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[39]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terrorist_attack">Terrorist attack</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Terrorist attack">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/1996_Manchester_bombing" title="1996 Manchester bombing">A terrorist attack took place in Manchester</a> on 15 June, one day before the group stage match between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_national_football_team" title="German national football team">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russia_national_football_team" title="Russia national football team">Russia</a> was due to take place in the same city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[42]</a></sup> The detonation of a <a href="/wiki/Car_bomb" title="Car bomb">van bomb</a> in the city centre injured 212 people and caused an estimated £700 million worth of damage. Four days after the blast, the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army">Provisional Irish Republican Army</a> issued a statement in which it claimed responsibility, but regretted causing injury to civilians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Manchester bombing was the first and so far only major terrorist attack in the host city of an ongoing UEFA European Championship. The scheduled match at <a href="/wiki/Old_Trafford" title="Old Trafford">Old Trafford</a> on the day following the bombing went ahead as planned after the stadium had been heavily guarded overnight and carefully searched; the game, in which <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro_1996_Group_C#Russia_vs_Germany" title="UEFA Euro 1996 Group C">Germany defeated Russia 3–0</a>, was watched by a near capacity crowd of 50,700.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disorder">Disorder</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=UEFA_Euro_1996&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Disorder">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro_1996_knockout_stage#England_vs_Germany" title="UEFA Euro 1996 knockout stage">England's defeat to Germany in the semi-finals</a>, a large-scale riot took place in <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a> and the surrounding area. Further outbreaks of trouble occurred in the streets of several other towns. The police, German-made cars were targeted, with damage also caused to various other properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riot_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Riot-49">[44]</a></sup> A Russian student was stabbed in <a href="/wiki/Brighton" title="Brighton">Brighton</a> after attackers mistook him for being German.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite this outbreak, the tournament overall was free of hooliganism, helping rehabilitate England's reputation after their fans' conduct during the previous decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riot_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Riot-49">[44]</a></sup> UEFA's awarding of the tournament to England was in itself a further step in bringing the country back fully into the international fold, coming soon after their decision in 1990 to re-admit English clubs back into UEFA competitions after the indefinite ban issued to them following the <a href="/wiki/Heysel_Stadium_disaster" title="Heysel Stadium disaster">Heysel Stadium disaster</a> in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[47]</a></sup></p>
